often than not, descriptions of indie-folk group Daughter rely on words like \"gloom,\" \"moody\" and \"devastating.\" So much so that the band's lead singer Elena Tonra said in an interview that she's been surprised by their reception considering their songs aren't exactly feel-good jams. But on their 2016 release, Not to Disappear, the band took a calculated risk and abandoned their affinity for the morose. Instead, Tonra explores the brighter primary colors of her voice and the band follows suit by pushing the songs to be faster and more energetic. Tonra doesn't lose the moodiness that defines the band, but she reinvents what Daughter is capable of. For a band that so heavily leaned on one sound for their debut record, this year's album could've been an identity crisis wallowing in uncertainty. But Tonra and her bandmates command their new sound like they've been waiting for this transformation this whole time. With a more clear leading voice and less bummerific song arrangements, Daughter might actually have people dancing instead of breaking out the handkerchiefs.                \n                    Lindsey Rhoades                \n            \n                                    British dream-pop trio Daughter have thrived while maintaining a tenuous balance Elena Tonra's\nbrittle vocals and bitter words might seem more at home in an acoustic folk setting, but backed by the shoegazey reverb of Igor Haefeli's guitars and Remi Aguilella's delirious drumming, Daughter delivers poignancy in a deafening dichotomy. On\nthe strength of just two EPs, His Young Heart and The Wild Youth (both 2011), the band was signed by venerated indie label 4AD, which put out their full-length debut, If You Leave, in 2013. Practically overnight, Daughter went from playing tiny venues (their first shows in New York were at Mercury Lounge and now-defunct DIY spot Glasslands) to enjoying high billing on the festival circuit (Coachella, Primavera, Panorama). The ephemeral nature of their music might suggest a fading away of sorts, but they've asserted themselves quite literally with their sophomore LP, Not to Disappear, upping the ante production-wise and adding bass and keys on tour. They debuted the album — recorded at Greenpoint studio Rare Book Room — earlier this year at small-scale not-for-profit venue National Sawdust; they'll unleash the record's full power once again here,
